      <p>
       <b>
        East Harlem
       </b>
       , also known as
       <b>
        Spanish Harlem
       </b>
       or
       <b>
        El Barrio
       </b>
       , is a neighborhood of
       <a href="/wiki/Upper_Manhattan" title="Upper Manhattan">
        Upper Manhattan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">
        New York City
       </a>
       roughly encompassing the area north of the
       <a href="/wiki/Upper_East_Side" title="Upper East Side">
        Upper East Side
       </a>
       and East
       <a href="/wiki/96th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="96th Street (Manhattan)">
        96th Street
       </a>
       up to about the 140s,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hot_3-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-hot-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       east of
       <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Avenue" title="Fifth Avenue">
        Fifth Avenue
       </a>
       to the
       <a href="/wiki/East_River" title="East River">
        East
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Harlem_River" title="Harlem River">
        Harlem Rivers
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It lies within
       <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Community_Board_11" title="Manhattan Community Board 11">
        Manhattan Community District 11
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyc.gov_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nyc.gov-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Despite its name, it is generally not considered to be a part of
       <a href="/wiki/Harlem" title="Harlem">
        Harlem
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The neighborhood is one of the largest predominantly
       <a href="/wiki/Latino" title="Latino">
        Latino
       </a>
       communities in New York City, mostly made up of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_people" title="Puerto Rican people">
        Puerto Ricans
       </a>
       , as well as sizeable numbers of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dominican_American_(Dominican_Republic)" title="Dominican American (Dominican Republic)">
        Dominican
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuban_American" title="Cuban American">
        Cuban
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexican_people" title="Mexican people">
        Mexican
       </a>
       immigrants. It includes the area formerly known as
       <b>
        Italian Harlem
       </b>
       , in which the remnants of a once predominantly
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_American" title="Italian American">
        Italian community
       </a>
       remain. The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_Americans_in_New_York_City" title="Chinese Americans in New York City">
        Chinese
       </a>
       population has increased dramatically in East Harlem since 2000.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       East Harlem has historically suffered from many social issues, such as the highest
       <a href="/wiki/Unemployment" title="Unemployment">
        jobless rate
       </a>
       in New York City,
       <a href="/wiki/Teenage_pregnancy" title="Teenage pregnancy">
        teenage pregnancy
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AIDS" title="AIDS">
        AIDS
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drug_abuse" title="Drug abuse">
        drug abuse
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Homelessness" title="Homelessness">
        homelessness
       </a>
       , and an
       <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">
        asthma
       </a>
       rate five times the national average.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyc.gov_5-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-nyc.gov-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It has the second highest concentration of
       <a href="/wiki/Public_housing_in_the_United_States" title="Public housing in the United States">
        public housing
       </a>
       in the United States, closely following
       <a href="/wiki/Brownsville,_Brooklyn" title="Brownsville, Brooklyn">
        Brownsville, Brooklyn
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Police services to the neighborhood are divided between the 23rd and the 25th Precincts.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Conversely, East Harlem is notable for its contributions to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_freestyle" title="Latin freestyle">
        Latin freestyle
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Salsa_music" title="Salsa music">
        salsa music
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       However, East Harlem is undergoing some
       <a href="/wiki/Gentrification" title="Gentrification">
        gentrification
       </a>
       . In February 2016, East Harlem was one of four neighborhoods featured in an article in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">
         The New York Times
        </a>
       </i>
       about "New Hot Neighborhoods", and the city was considering re-zoning the area.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3">
           <a href="#Italian_Harlem">
            <span class="tocnumber">
             1.2
            </span>
            <span class="toctext">
             Italian Harlem
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4">

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Harlem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Harlem" title="History of Harlem">
        History of Harlem
       </a>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">
        Early history
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Harlem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early history">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The area which became East Harlem was rural for most of the 19th century, but residential settlements northeast of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_Avenue_(Manhattan)" title="Third Avenue (Manhattan)">
        Third Avenue
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/110th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="110th Street (Manhattan)">
        East 110th Street
       </a>
       had developed by the 1860s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encnyc_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-encnyc-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The construction of the
       <a href="/wiki/Rapid_transit" title="Rapid transit">
        elevated transit line
       </a>
       to Harlem in 1879 and 1880, and the building of the
       <a href="/wiki/IRT_Lexington_Avenue_Line" title="IRT Lexington Avenue Line">
        Lexington Avenue subway
       </a>
       in 1919,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encnyc_13-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-encnyc-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       urbanized the area, precipitating the construction of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apartment_buildings" title="Apartment buildings">
        apartment buildings
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brownstones" title="Brownstones">
        brownstones
       </a>
       . The extension of cable cars up Lexington Avenue into East Harlem was stymied by the incline created by
       <a href="/wiki/Duffy%27s_Hill" title="Duffy's Hill">
        Duffy's Hill
       </a>
       at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/103rd_Street_(Manhattan)" title="103rd Street (Manhattan)">
        103rd Street
       </a>
       , one of the steepest grades in Manhattan. East Harlem was first populated by poor
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_people" title="German people">
        German
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">
        Irish
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scandinavian_people" title="Scandinavian people">
        Scandinavian
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aia_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-aia-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_European" title="Eastern European">
        Eastern European
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Jews_in_New_York_City" title="Jews in New York City">
        Jewish
       </a>
       immigrants, with the Jewish population standing at 90,000 around 1917.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encnyc_13-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-encnyc-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the 1870s, Italian immigrants joined the mix after a contractor building trolley tracks on First Avenue imported Italian laborers as
       <a href="/wiki/Strikebreaker" title="Strikebreaker">
        strikebreakers
       </a>
       . The workers' shantytown along the East River at 106th Street was the beginning of an Italian neighborhood, with 4,000 having arrived by the mid-1880s. As more immigrants arrived, it expanded north to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/115th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="115th Street (Manhattan)">
        East 115th Street
       </a>
       and west to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_Avenue_(Manhattan)" title="Third Avenue (Manhattan)">
        Third Avenue
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gotham1123_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-gotham1123-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       East Harlem now consisted of pockets of ethnically-sorted settlements – Italian, German, Irish, and Jewish – which were beginning to press up against each other, with the spaces still between them occupied by "gasworks, stockyards and tar and garbage dumps".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gotham1123_15-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-gotham1123-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1895,
       <a href="/wiki/Union_Settlement_Association" title="Union Settlement Association">
        Union Settlement Association
       </a>
       , one of the oldest settlement houses in New York City, began providing services in the area, offering the immigrant and low-income residents a range of community-based programs, including boys and girls clubs, a sewing school and adult education classes.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:377px;max-width:377px">
        <div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent">
         Vestiges of Italian Harlem
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:197px;max-width:197px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lady_Mt_Carmel_RCC_sunny_jeh.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="4224" data-file-width="3168" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Lady_Mt_Carmel_RCC_sunny_jeh.jpg/195px-Lady_Mt_Carmel_RCC_sunny_jeh.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Lady_Mt_Carmel_RCC_sunny_jeh.jpg/293px-Lady_Mt_Carmel_RCC_sunny_jeh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Lady_Mt_Carmel_RCC_sunny_jeh.jpg/390px-Lady_Mt_Carmel_RCC_sunny_jeh.jpg 2x" width="195"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Our_Lady_of_Mt._Carmel_(Manhattan)" title="Church of Our Lady of Mt. Carmel (Manhattan)">
           Church of Our Lady of Mt. Carmel
          </a>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:176px;max-width:176px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NYC-East_Harlem-Patsys_Pizzeria-01.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="3872" data-file-width="2592" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/NYC-East_Harlem-Patsys_Pizzeria-01.jpg/174px-NYC-East_Harlem-Patsys_Pizzeria-01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/NYC-East_Harlem-Patsys_Pizzeria-01.jpg/261px-NYC-East_Harlem-Patsys_Pizzeria-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/NYC-East_Harlem-Patsys_Pizzeria-01.jpg/348px-NYC-East_Harlem-Patsys_Pizzeria-01.jpg 2x" width="174"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          <a href="/wiki/Patsy%27s_Pizzeria" title="Patsy's Pizzeria">
           Patsy's Pizzeria
          </a>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div style="clear:left">
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Italian_Harlem">
        Italian Harlem
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Harlem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Italian Harlem">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_Italy" title="Southern Italy">
        Southern Italians
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sicilian_people" title="Sicilian people">
        Sicilians
       </a>
       , with a moderate number of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Italy" title="North Italy">
        Northern Italians
       </a>
       , soon predominated, especially in the area east of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lexington_Avenue_(Manhattan)" title="Lexington Avenue (Manhattan)">
        Lexington Avenue
       </a>
       between 96th and
       <a href="/wiki/116th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="116th Street (Manhattan)">
        116th
       </a>
       Streets and east of
       <a href="/wiki/Madison_Avenue" title="Madison Avenue">
        Madison Avenue
       </a>
       between 116th and
       <a href="/wiki/125th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="125th Street (Manhattan)">
        125th
       </a>
       Streets, with each street featuring people from different regions of Italy. The neighborhood became known as "Italian Harlem", the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_American" title="Italian American">
        Italian American
       </a>
       hub of Manhattan; it was the first part of Manhattan to be referred to as "
       <a href="/wiki/Little_Italy,_Manhattan" title="Little Italy, Manhattan">
        Little Italy
       </a>
       ".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The first Italians arrived in East Harlem in 1878, from
       <a href="/wiki/Polla" title="Polla">
        Polla
       </a>
       in the province of Salerno, and settled in the vicinity of 115th Street.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meyer_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-meyer-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There were many crime syndicates in Italian Harlem from the early
       <a href="/wiki/Black_Hand_(extortion)" title="Black Hand (extortion)">
        Black Hand
       </a>
       to the bigger and more organized Italian gangs that formed the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian-American_Mafia" title="Italian-American Mafia">
        Italian-American Mafia
       </a>
       . It was the founding location of the
       <a href="/wiki/Genovese_crime_family" title="Genovese crime family">
        Genovese crime family
       </a>
       , one of the
       <a href="/wiki/Five_Families" title="Five Families">
        Five Families
       </a>
       that dominated organized crime in New York City.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This includes the current
       <a href="/wiki/116th_Street_Crew" title="116th Street Crew">
        116th Street Crew
       </a>
       of the Genovese family. During the 1970s, Italian East Harlem was also home to the Italian-American drug gang and murder-for-hire crew known as the
       <a href="/wiki/East_Harlem_Purple_Gang" title="East Harlem Purple Gang">
        East Harlem Purple Gang
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In the 1920s and early 1930s, Italian Harlem was represented in Congress by future Mayor
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fiorello_La_Guardia" title="Fiorello La Guardia">
        Fiorello La Guardia
       </a>
       , and later, in the 1940s, by Italian-American civil rights lawyer, activist, and
       <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">
        socialist
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Vito_Marcantonio" title="Vito Marcantonio">
        Vito Marcantonio
       </a>
       . The Italian neighborhood approached its peak in the 1930s, with over 100,000 Italian-Americans living in its crowded, run-down apartment buildings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The 1930 census showed that 81 percent of the population of Italian Harlem consisted of first- or second-generation Italian Americans. (Somewhat less than the concentration of Italian Americans in the
       <a href="/wiki/Lower_East_Side" title="Lower East Side">
        Lower East Side
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/Little_Italy,_Manhattan" title="Little Italy, Manhattan">
        Little Italy
       </a>
       with 88 percent; Italian Harlem's total population, however, was three times that of Little Italy.)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meyer_17-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-meyer-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Italian community in East Harlem remained strong into the 1980s, but it has slowly diminished since then. However, Italian inhabitants and vestiges of the old Italian neighborhood remain. The annual Feast of Our Lady of Mount Carmel and the "Dancing of the Giglio", the first Italian feast in New York City, is still celebrated there every year on the second weekend of August by the
       <a href="/wiki/Giglio_Society_of_East_Harlem" title="Giglio Society of East Harlem">
        Giglio Society of East Harlem
       </a>
       . Italian retail establishments still exist, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Rao%27s" title="Rao's">
        Rao's
       </a>
       restaurant, started in 1896, and the original
       <a href="/wiki/Patsy%27s_Pizzeria" title="Patsy's Pizzeria">
        Patsy's Pizzeria
       </a>
       which opened in the 1933. In May 2011, one of the last remaining Italian retail businesses in the neighborhood, a barbershop owned by Claudio Caponigro on 116th Street, was threatened with closure by a rent increase.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berger2_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Berger2-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish_Harlem">
        Spanish Harlem
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Harlem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Spanish Harlem">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_people" title="Puerto Rican people">
        Puerto Rican
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">
        Latin American
       </a>
       immigration after the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">
        First World War
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fednyc_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-fednyc-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       established an enclave at the western portion of East Harlem – around 110th Street and Lexington Avenue – which became known as "Spanish Harlem". The area slowly grew to encompass all of East Harlem, including Italian Harlem, as Italians moved out – to
       <a href="/wiki/The_Bronx" title="The Bronx">
        the Bronx
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn" title="Brooklyn">
        Brooklyn
       </a>
       , upstate
       <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">
        New York
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">
        New Jersey
       </a>
       – and Hispanics moved in during another wave of immigration in the 1940s and 1950s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encnyc_13-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-encnyc-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although in certain areas, particularly around
       <a href="/wiki/Pleasant_Avenue" title="Pleasant Avenue">
        Pleasant Avenue
       </a>
       , Italian Harlem lasted through the 1970s,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berger_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Berger-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       today most of the former Italian population is gone. Most of these predominantly older residents are clustered around Our Lady of Mount Carmel Church, mainly from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/114th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="114th Street (Manhattan)">
        114th
       </a>
       to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/118th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="118th Street (Manhattan)">
        118th
       </a>
       Streets. According to the
       <a href="/wiki/2000_United_States_Census" title="2000 United States Census">
        2000 Census
       </a>
       , there were only 1,130 Italian-Americans still living in this area.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The newly dominant Puerto Rican population, which reached 63,000 in 1950, continued to define the neighborhood according to its needs, establishing
       <a href="/wiki/Convenience_store" title="Convenience store">
        bodegas
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Bot%C3%A1nica" title="Botánica">
        botánicas
       </a>
       as it expanded; by the 1930s
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fednyc_21-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-fednyc-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       there was already an enclosed street market underneath the Park Avenue railroad viaduct between 111th and 116th Streets, called "La Marqueta" ("The Market").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encnyc_13-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-encnyc-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Catholic and evangelistic Protestant churches appeared in storefronts.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encnyc_13-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-encnyc-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although "Spanish Harlem" had been in use since at least the 1930s to describe the Hispanic enclave – along with "Italian Harlem" and "Negro Harlem"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       – the name began to be used to describe the entire East Harlem neighborhood by the 1950s. Later, the name "El Barrio" ("The Neighborhood") began to be used, especially by inhabitants of the area.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Decline">
        Decline
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Harlem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Decline">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In the 1950s and 1960s, large sections of East Harlem were leveled for urban renewal projects, and the neighborhood was one of the hardest hit areas in the 1960s and 1970s as New York City struggled with deficits,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Race_riots" title="Race riots">
        race riots
       </a>
       , urban flight, gang warfare, drug abuse, crime and poverty. Tenements were crowded, poorly maintained, and frequent targets for arson. In 1969 and 1970, a regional chapter of the
       <a href="/wiki/Young_Lords" title="Young Lords">
        Young Lords
       </a>
       which were reorganized from a neighborhood street gang in Chicago by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jose_(Cha-Cha)_Jimenez" title="Jose (Cha-Cha) Jimenez">
        Jose (Cha-Cha) Jimenez
       </a>
       , ran several programs including a Free Breakfast for Children and a Free Health Clinic to help Latino and poor families. The Young Lords came together with the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_Panthers" title="Black Panthers">
        Black Panthers
       </a>
       and called for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_independence_movement" title="Puerto Rican independence movement">
        Puerto Rican independence
       </a>
       and neighborhood empowerment. Still, as of the early 2000s, the
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_Kings_(gang)" title="Latin Kings (gang)">
        Latin Kings
       </a>
       gang was still prevalent in East Harlem.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_history">
        Recent history
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Harlem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Recent history">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       By the beginning of the 21st century, East Harlem was a racially diverse neighborhood, with about a third of the population being Puerto Rican.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encnyc_13-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-encnyc-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As it has been throughout its history, it is predominantly a working-class neighborhood.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aia_14-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-aia-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Until 2006, property values in East Harlem climbed along with those in the rest of New York City. With increased market-rate housing, including luxury condos and co-ops – most built on formerly vacant lots – there has been some decline of affordable housing in the community. A number of young professionals have settled into these recently constructed buildings. This influx of "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuppies" title="Yuppies">
        yuppies
       </a>
       " has caused rents to rise, more buildings in the area to get gut renovations, and changes to area demographics.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On March 12, 2014 at 9:00
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Daylight_Time" title="Eastern Daylight Time">
        EDT
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/2014_East_Harlem_gas_explosion" title="2014 East Harlem gas explosion">
        a large explosion
       </a>
       and fire at 1644–1646
       <a href="/wiki/Park_Avenue" title="Park Avenue">
        Park Avenue
       </a>
       killed eight people and injured more than 70.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/New_York_Post" title="New York Post">
         New York Post
        </a>
       </i>
       listed one part of the neighborhood – the block of
       <a href="/wiki/Lexington_Avenue" title="Lexington Avenue">
        Lexington Avenue
       </a>
       between East 123rd and 124th Streets – as one of "the most dangerous blocks in the city" because police crime statistics for 2015 showed that 19 assaults had taken place there, more than for any other city block. The
       <i>
        Post
       </i>
       also reported that there were, according to the Harlem Neighborhood Block Association, "22 drug-treatment programs, four homeless-services providers and four transitional-living facilities" in East Harlem.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       East Harlem has begun to feel the effects of
       <a href="/wiki/Gentrification" title="Gentrification">
        gentrification
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In February 2016, an article in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">
         The New York Times
        </a>
       </i>
       about "New York's Next Hot Neighborhoods" featured East Harlem as one of four such areas. A real-estate broker described it as "one of the few remaining areas in New York City where you can secure a good deal". The article mentioned new luxury developments, access to transportation, the opening of new retail stores, bars and restaurants, and national-brand stores beginning to appear on the outskirts of the neighborhood. Primarily, though, it was the cost of housing in comparison to the rest of Manhattan, which the article noted as the major factor.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hot_3-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-hot-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Beginning in 2016, the New York City government was seeking to rezone East Harlem "to facilitate new residential, commercial, community facility, and manufacturing development".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The residents of the neighborhood generated a suggested zoning plan, the "East Harlem Neighborhood Plan", which was offered to the city in February 2017,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       , but in August 2017 residents and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manhattan_Borough_President" title="Manhattan Borough President">
        Manhattan Borough President
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Gale_Brewer" title="Gale Brewer">
        Gale Brewer
       </a>
       , complained that the city had ignored their plan almost entirely.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">
        Demographics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Harlem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Demographics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elbarrio116thLex.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1089" data-file-width="1440" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Elbarrio116thLex.jpg/275px-Elbarrio116thLex.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Elbarrio116thLex.jpg/413px-Elbarrio116thLex.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Elbarrio116thLex.jpg/550px-Elbarrio116thLex.jpg 2x" width="275"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elbarrio116thLex.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Storefronts at
         <a href="/wiki/Lexington_Avenue" title="Lexington Avenue">
          Lexington Avenue
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/116th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="116th Street (Manhattan)">
          116th Street
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Department_of_City_Planning" title="New York City Department of City Planning">
        New York City Department of City Planning
       </a>
       calculated data for East Harlem North and East Harlem South from the 2010 Census. The two sub-neighborhoods had a combined population of 115,921, an increase of 1,874 (1.4%) from the combined 114,047 in the
       <a href="/wiki/2000_United_States_Census" title="2000 United States Census">
        2000 Census
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PLP5North_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-PLP5North-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PLP5South_2-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-PLP5South-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Based on data from the
       <a href="/wiki/2010_United_States_Census" title="2010 United States Census">
        2010 United States Census
       </a>
       , the population of East Harlem North was 58,019, an increase of 871 (1.5%) from the 57,148 counted in 2000. Covering an area of 573.94 acres (232.27 ha), the neighborhood had a population density of 101.1 inhabitants per acre (64,700/sq mi; 25,000/km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PLP5North_1-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-PLP5North-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The racial makeup of the neighborhood was 6.8% (3,936)
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_(U.S._Census)" title="White (U.S. Census)">
        White
       </a>
       , 35.5% (20,625)
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American_(U.S._Census)" title="African American (U.S. Census)">
        African American
       </a>
       , 0.2% (128)
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_American_(U.S._Census)" title="Native American (U.S. Census)">
        Native American
       </a>
       , 3.0% (1,766)
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_(U.S._Census)" title="Asian (U.S. Census)">
        Asian
       </a>
       , 0.0% (9)
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific_Islander_(U.S._Census)" title="Pacific Islander (U.S. Census)">
        Pacific Islander
       </a>
       , 0.3% (185) from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Race_(United_States_Census)" title="Race (United States Census)">
        other races
       </a>
       , and 1.3% (769) from two or more races.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hispanic_(U.S._Census)" title="Hispanic (U.S. Census)">
        Hispanic
       </a>
       or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latino_(U.S._Census)" title="Latino (U.S. Census)">
        Latino
       </a>
       of any race were 52.7% (30,601) of the population.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PLP3ANorth_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PLP3ANorth-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Based on data from the 2010 Census, the population of East Harlem South was 57,902, an increase of 1,003 (1.8%) from the 56,899 counted in 2000. Covering an area of 389.41 acres (157.59 ha), the neighborhood had a population density of 148.7 inhabitants per acre (95,200/sq mi; 36,700/km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PLP5South_2-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-PLP5South-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The racial makeup of the neighborhood was 17.4% (10,072)
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_(U.S._Census)" title="White (U.S. Census)">
        White
       </a>
       , 24.6% (14,227)
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American_(U.S._Census)" title="African American (U.S. Census)">
        African American
       </a>
       , 0.2% (96)
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_American_(U.S._Census)" title="Native American (U.S. Census)">
        Native American
       </a>
       , 8.3% (4,802)
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_(U.S._Census)" title="Asian (U.S. Census)">
        Asian
       </a>
       , 0.1% (55)
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific_Islander_(U.S._Census)" title="Pacific Islander (U.S. Census)">
        Pacific Islander
       </a>
       , 0.4% (218) from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Race_(United_States_Census)" title="Race (United States Census)">
        other races
       </a>
       , and 1.6% (933) from two or more races.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hispanic_(U.S._Census)" title="Hispanic (U.S. Census)">
        Hispanic
       </a>
       or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latino_(U.S._Census)" title="Latino (U.S. Census)">
        Latino
       </a>
       of any race were 47.5% (27,499) of the population.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PLP3ASouth_35-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PLP3ASouth-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Manhattan Community District 11, which covers East Harlem in its entirety, is a mostly low and moderate income area. It is made up of first and second generation
       <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Ricans" title="Puerto Ricans">
        Puerto Ricans
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African-Americans" title="African-Americans">
        African-Americans
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian-Americans" title="Italian-Americans">
        Italian-Americans
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian-Americans" title="Asian-Americans">
        Asian-Americans
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/West_Indian_Americans" title="West Indian Americans">
        West Indian Americans
       </a>
       (Especially
       <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Americans" title="Dominican Americans">
        Dominican Americans
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Americans" title="Cuban Americans">
        Cuban Americans
       </a>
       ), and a growing population of
       <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Americans" title="Mexican Americans">
        Mexican Americans
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Salvadoran_Americans" title="Salvadoran Americans">
        Salvadoran Americans
       </a>
       and other
       <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">
        Central American
       </a>
       immigrants.
      </p>
      <p>
       It has one of the highest concentrations of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_American" title="Puerto Rican American">
        Puerto Ricans
       </a>
       in all of New York City. In the 2000 Census, 52.1% of residents described themselves as of Hispanic origin, 35.7 as non-Hispanic black, 7.3 as non-Hispanic white, 2.7 Asian and Pacific Islander Nonhispanic, 1.7% as two or more non-Hispanic races, and 0.5% as another race. By New York City averages, the youth makes up a larger than normal percentage of the East Harlem population with 30.6% of residents age 18 or younger.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyc.gov_5-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-nyc.gov-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       93.6% of all housing units were renter-occupied, and over 25% of the population resided in public housing units managed by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NYCHA" title="NYCHA">
        NYCHA
       </a>
       . 46.5 percent of the population receive a form of income support by the government.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyc.gov_5-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-nyc.gov-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       According to a 2010 study, the number of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_American" title="Asian American">
        Asians
       </a>
       in East Harlem nearly tripled between 2000 and 2010, largely due to Chinese people moving to East Harlem. Increasing rents in
       <a href="/wiki/Chinatown,_Manhattan" title="Chinatown, Manhattan">
        Lower Manhattan's Chinatown
       </a>
       have driven many into public and subsidized housing developments in the neighborhood. Advocates have been calling for Chinese language services to be available in the community centers to accommodate the growing number of Chinese residents in the area. In 2000, the Chinese population in the northern portion was less than one percent, but by 2010, it has gone up to being three percent in the area. In the southern part, it rose from 4.6% to 8.4%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As of 2010, the Puerto Rican population was 27.7% in zip code 10029,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and 23.4% in 10035. 10035 also has a large Mexican population, at 10.7%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">
        Transportation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Harlem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Transportation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HARLEM_RIVER-HARLEM_ON_LEFT._BRONX_ON_RIGHT_-_NARA_-_548427.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2019" data-file-width="3000" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/HARLEM_RIVER-HARLEM_ON_LEFT._BRONX_ON_RIGHT_-_NARA_-_548427.jpg/275px-HARLEM_RIVER-HARLEM_ON_LEFT._BRONX_ON_RIGHT_-_NARA_-_548427.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/HARLEM_RIVER-HARLEM_ON_LEFT._BRONX_ON_RIGHT_-_NARA_-_548427.jpg/413px-HARLEM_RIVER-HARLEM_ON_LEFT._BRONX_ON_RIGHT_-_NARA_-_548427.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/HARLEM_RIVER-HARLEM_ON_LEFT._BRONX_ON_RIGHT_-_NARA_-_548427.jpg/550px-HARLEM_RIVER-HARLEM_ON_LEFT._BRONX_ON_RIGHT_-_NARA_-_548427.jpg 2x" width="275"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HARLEM_RIVER-HARLEM_ON_LEFT._BRONX_ON_RIGHT_-_NARA_-_548427.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Bridges spanning the
         <a href="/wiki/Harlem_River" title="Harlem River">
          Harlem River
         </a>
         between Harlem to the left and the Bronx to the right
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MNRR_NH-Line-Train_125th-St.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="845" data-file-width="1126" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/MNRR_NH-Line-Train_125th-St.jpg/275px-MNRR_NH-Line-Train_125th-St.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/MNRR_NH-Line-Train_125th-St.jpg/413px-MNRR_NH-Line-Train_125th-St.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/MNRR_NH-Line-Train_125th-St.jpg/550px-MNRR_NH-Line-Train_125th-St.jpg 2x" width="275"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MNRR_NH-Line-Train_125th-St.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harlem_%E2%80%93_125th_Street_(Metro-North_station)" title="Harlem – 125th Street (Metro-North station)">
          Harlem – 125th Street
         </a>
         on the
         <a href="/wiki/Metro-North_Railroad" title="Metro-North Railroad">
          Metro-North Railroad
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Harlem_River" title="Harlem River">
        Harlem River
       </a>
       separates
       <a href="/wiki/The_Bronx" title="The Bronx">
        the Bronx
       </a>
       and Manhattan, necessitating several spans between the two New York City boroughs. In East Harlem, the
       <a href="/wiki/Wards_Island_Bridge" title="Wards Island Bridge">
        Wards Island Bridge
       </a>
       , also known as the 103rd Street Footbridge, connects Manhattan with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wards_Island" title="Wards Island">
        Wards Island
       </a>
       . The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triboro_Bridge" title="Triboro Bridge">
        Triboro Bridge
       </a>
       is a complex of three separate bridges that offers connections between
       <a href="/wiki/Queens" title="Queens">
        Queens
       </a>
       , Manhattan (Harlem), and the Bronx.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Public transportation service is provided by the
       <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Transportation_Authority" title="Metropolitan Transportation Authority">
        Metropolitan Transportation Authority
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Subway" title="New York City Subway">
        New York City Subway
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/IRT_Lexington_Avenue_Line" title="IRT Lexington Avenue Line">
        IRT Lexington Avenue Line
       </a>
       runs through East Harlem, with an express station at
       <a href="/wiki/125th_Street_(IRT_Lexington_Avenue_Line)" title="125th Street (IRT Lexington Avenue Line)">
        125th Street
       </a>
       (served by the
       <span class="nowrap">
        <a href="/wiki/4_(New_York_City_Subway_service)" title="4 (New York City Subway service)">
         4
        </a>
       </span>
       , ​
       <span class="nowrap">
        <a href="/wiki/5_(New_York_City_Subway_service)" title="5 (New York City Subway service)">
         5
        </a>
       </span>
       , ​
       <span class="nowrap">
        <a href="/wiki/6_(New_York_City_Subway_service)" title="6 (New York City Subway service)">
         6
        </a>
       </span>
       , and
       <span class="nowrap">
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/6d_(New_York_City_Subway_service)" title="6d (New York City Subway service)">
         &lt;6&gt;
        </a>
       </span>
       routes) as well as local stations at the
       <a href="/wiki/116th_Street_(IRT_Lexington_Avenue_Line)" title="116th Street (IRT Lexington Avenue Line)">
        116th Street
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/110th_Street_(IRT_Lexington_Avenue_Line)" title="110th Street (IRT Lexington Avenue Line)">
        110th Street
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/103rd_Street_(IRT_Lexington_Avenue_Line)" title="103rd Street (IRT Lexington Avenue Line)">
        103rd Street
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/96th_Street_(IRT_Lexington_Avenue_Line)" title="96th Street (IRT Lexington Avenue Line)">
        96th Street
       </a>
       (served by the
       <span class="nowrap">
        <a href="/wiki/6_(New_York_City_Subway_service)" title="6 (New York City Subway service)">
         6
        </a>
       </span>
       and
       <span class="nowrap">
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/6d_(New_York_City_Subway_service)" title="6d (New York City Subway service)">
         &lt;6&gt;
        </a>
       </span>
       ​ routes). There is also a
       <a href="/wiki/Second_Avenue_Subway" title="Second Avenue Subway">
        Second Avenue Subway
       </a>
       station at
       <a href="/wiki/96th_Street_(Second_Avenue_Subway)" title="96th Street (Second Avenue Subway)">
        96th Street
       </a>
       on the
       <span class="nowrap">
        <a href="/wiki/Q_(New_York_City_Subway_service)" title="Q (New York City Subway service)">
         Q
        </a>
       </span>
       route.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/MTA_Regional_Bus_Operations" title="MTA Regional Bus Operations">
        MTA Regional Bus Operations
       </a>
       <span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">
        '
       </span>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M15_(New_York_City_bus)" title="M15 (New York City bus)">
        M15
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M15_SBS_(New_York_City_bus)" title="M15 SBS (New York City bus)">
        M15 SBS
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M35_(New_York_City_bus)" title="M35 (New York City bus)">
        M35
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M60_SBS_(New_York_City_bus)" title="M60 SBS (New York City bus)">
        M60 SBS
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M96_(New_York_City_bus)" title="M96 (New York City bus)">
        M96
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M98_(New_York_City_bus)" title="M98 (New York City bus)">
        M98
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M100_(New_York_City_bus)" title="M100 (New York City bus)">
        M100
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M101_(New_York_City_bus)" title="M101 (New York City bus)">
        M101
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M102_(New_York_City_bus)" title="M102 (New York City bus)">
        M102
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M103_(New_York_City_bus)" title="M103 (New York City bus)">
        M103
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M116_(New_York_City_bus)" title="M116 (New York City bus)">
        M116
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bx15_(New_York_City_bus)" title="Bx15 (New York City bus)">
        Bx15
       </a>
       bus routes serve East Harlem as well.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Metro-North_Railroad" title="Metro-North Railroad">
        Metro-North Railroad
       </a>
       has a
       <a href="/wiki/Commuter_rail" title="Commuter rail">
        commuter rail
       </a>
       station at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harlem_%E2%80%93_125th_Street_(Metro-North_station)" title="Harlem – 125th Street (Metro-North station)">
        Harlem – 125th Street
       </a>
       , connecting to
       <a href="/wiki/Westchester_County,_New_York" title="Westchester County, New York">
        Westchester County
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">
        Connecticut
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Social_issues">
        Social issues
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Harlem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Social issues">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Social problems, including poverty, crime, and drug addiction, have plagued the area since the 1970s. Although crime rates have dropped from the historically high numbers of the past, East Harlem suffers from Manhattan's highest violent crime rate, with 15 murders in 2011.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       East Harlem has the highest concentration of shelters and facilities in Manhattan, with eight homeless shelters, 36 drug and alcohol treatment facilities and 37 mental health treatment facilities. It also has the highest jobless rate in the entire city, as well as the city's second highest cumulative AIDS rate. The asthma rate is also 5 times larger than national levels.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyc.gov_5-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-nyc.gov-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The neighborhood also suffers from a high poverty rate.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Union_Settlement_Association" title="Union Settlement Association">
        Union Settlement Association
       </a>
       is one of the neighborhood's largest social service agencies, reaching more than 13,000 people annually at 17 locations throughout East Harlem, through a range of programs, including early childhood education, youth development, senior services, job training, the arts,
       <a href="/wiki/Adult_education" title="Adult education">
        adult education
       </a>
       , nutrition, counseling, a farmers' market, community development, and neighborhood cultural events.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Super_Associated_East_Harlem_1968_2nd_Av_101_St_jeh.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2752" data-file-width="3800" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Super_Associated_East_Harlem_1968_2nd_Av_101_St_jeh.jpg/250px-Super_Associated_East_Harlem_1968_2nd_Av_101_St_jeh.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Super_Associated_East_Harlem_1968_2nd_Av_101_St_jeh.jpg/375px-Super_Associated_East_Harlem_1968_2nd_Av_101_St_jeh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Super_Associated_East_Harlem_1968_2nd_Av_101_St_jeh.jpg/500px-Super_Associated_East_Harlem_1968_2nd_Av_101_St_jeh.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Super_Associated_East_Harlem_1968_2nd_Av_101_St_jeh.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Associated_Supermarkets" title="Associated Supermarkets">
          Associated Supermarkets
         </a>
         grocery on East 101st Street
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Fresh_food">
        Fresh food
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Harlem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Fresh food">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       A lack of access to healthy food causes serious hardships to citizens of East Harlem, a neighborhood which is considered to be a
       <a href="/wiki/Food_desert" title="Food desert">
        food desert
       </a>
       . According to an April 2008 report prepared by the
       <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Department_of_City_Planning" title="New York City Department of City Planning">
        New York City Department of City Planning
       </a>
       , East Harlem is an area of the city with the highest levels of diet-related diseases due to limited opportunities for citizens to purchase fresh foods.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       With a high population density and a lack of nearby
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supermarkets" title="Supermarkets">
        supermarkets
       </a>
       , the neighborhood has little access to fresh fruit and vegetables and a low consumption of fresh foods. Citizens of East Harlem are likely to buy food from grocery stores that have a limited supply of fruits and vegetables, which are often of poor quality and generally more expensive than the same products sold at supermarkets. Compared to the
       <a href="/wiki/Upper_East_Side" title="Upper East Side">
        Upper East Side
       </a>
       , supermarkets in Harlem are 30% less common.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Without access to affordable produce and meats, East Harlem residents have difficulty eating a healthy diet, which contributes to high rates of obesity and diabetes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2011, Manhattan Borough President
       <a href="/wiki/Scott_Stringer" title="Scott Stringer">
        Scott Stringer
       </a>
       announced a program which would send Veggie Vans to East Harlem senior centers and housing projects.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2012, Whole Foods announced two uptown locations, one being on 125th Street and Lenox Avenue, the other in the Upper East Side.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2010, Aldi's Grocery opened at the East River Plaza
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       located at E. 117th St. and FDR Drive, providing access to affordable food for East Harlem's residents. In 2013, a new Super FI Emperior Grocery store opened up in East Harlem on 103rd Street and Lexington Avenue.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div style="clear:left;">
      </div>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Housing">
        Housing
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Harlem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Housing">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       East Harlem is dominated by public housing complexes of various types, with a high concentration of older tenement buildings between these developments. The neighborhood contains the highest geographical concentration of low income public housing projects in the United States. The total land area is 1.54 square miles (4.0 km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       After a wave of arson ravaged the low income communities of New York City throughout the 1970s, many of the residential structures in East Harlem were left seriously damaged or destroyed. By the late 1970s, the city began to rehabilitate many abandoned tenement style buildings and designate them as low income housing.
      </p>
      <p>
       Despite recent gentrification of the neighborhood, large numbers of apartment buildings have been deliberately kept vacant by their owners. Although the businesses on the ground floor are retained, landlords do not want to have the trouble involved in residential tenants. In some cases, landlords are waiting for a revived economy, warehousing the apartments so that they can rent them later at a higher rent.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sealed_Apartments_56-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sealed_Apartments-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2007, a survey of Manhattan's buildings found that 1,723 were significantly vacant, three-quarters of them north of 96th Street. A 1998 survey found that one-quarter of low-rise residential buildings on avenues or major cross streets in East Harlem had sealed-up residential floors, despite having commercial businesses on the ground floor.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sealed_Apartments_56-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sealed_Apartments-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Public_housing_projects">
        Public housing projects
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Harlem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Public housing projects">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       There are twenty-four
       <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Housing_Authority" title="New York City Housing Authority">
        New York City Housing Authority
       </a>
       developments located in East Harlem.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        335 East 111th Street; one 6-story building
       </li>
       <li>
        East 120th Street Rehab; one, 6-story rehabilitated tenement building
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/East_River" title="East River">
         East River
        </a>
        Houses; ten buildings, 6, 10 and 11 stories tall
       </li>
       <li>
        Edward Corsi Houses; one 16-story building
       </li>
       <li>
        Gaylord White Houses; one 20-story building
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_Carver" title="George Washington Carver">
         George Washington Carver
        </a>
        Houses; 13 buildings, 6 and 15 stories tall
       </li>
       <li>
        Governor
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dewitt_Clinton" title="Dewitt Clinton">
         Dewitt Clinton
        </a>
        Houses; six buildings, 9 and 18 stories tall
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Robinson" title="Jackie Robinson">
         Jackie Robinson
        </a>
        Houses; one 8-story building
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/James_Weldon_Johnson" title="James Weldon Johnson">
         James Weldon Johnson
        </a>
        Houses; ten 14-story buildings
       </li>
       <li>
        Lehman Village; four 20-story buildings
       </li>
       <li>
        Lexington Houses; four 14-story buildings
       </li>
       <li>
        Metro North Plaza; three buildings, 7, 8, and 11 stories tall
       </li>
       <li>
        Metro North Rehab; seventeen 6-story rehabilitated tenement buildings
       </li>
       <li>
        Milbank-Frawley; two rehabilitated tenement buildings 5 and 6 stories tall
       </li>
       <li>
        Morris Park Senior Citizens Home; one 9-story rehabilitated building
       </li>
       <li>
        Park Avenue-East 122nd, 123rd Streets; two 6-story buildings
       </li>
       <li>
        President
        <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">
         Abraham Lincoln
        </a>
        ; fourteen buildings, 6 and 14 stories tall
       </li>
       <li>
        President
        <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">
         George Washington
        </a>
        Houses; fourteen buildings, 12 and 14 stories tall
       </li>
       <li>
        President
        <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">
         Thomas Jefferson
        </a>
        Houses; eighteen buildings, 7, 13 and 14 stories tall
       </li>
       <li>
        President
        <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">
         Woodrow Wilson
        </a>
        Houses; three 20-story buildings
       </li>
       <li>
        Senator
        <a href="/wiki/Robert_A._Taft" title="Robert A. Taft">
         Robert A. Taft
        </a>
        ; nine 19-story buildings
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Wagner_Houses" title="Robert F. Wagner Houses">
         Robert F. Wagner Houses
        </a>
        ; twenty-two buildings, 7 and 16 stories tall
       </li>
       <li>
        U.P.A.C.A. (Upper Park Avenue Community Association) Site 6; one 12-story building
       </li>
       <li>
        U.P.A.C.A. (Upper Park Avenue Community Association) U.R.A. Site 5; one 11-story building
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Other subsidized housing include:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Taino Towers – East 122nd Street and Third Avenue. Four 35-story towers, 656 apartments. Opened 1979.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
         <a href="#cite_note-58">
          [58]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        A new 68-story rental tower has been proposed to be built in East Harlem, but is still in the process of gaining approval. The 1,300,000-square-foot (120,000 m
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
        ) building, which is currently the site of the School of Cooperative Technical Education, would house three schools and retail space along with a mix of 1,100 affordable and market-rate apartments.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
         <a href="#cite_note-59">
          [59]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Education_and_medical_services">
        Education and medical services
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Harlem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Education and medical services">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Harlem" title="Education in Harlem">
        Education in Harlem
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The schools in East Harlem are generally characterized by low test scores and high drop-out and truancy rates.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-streeteasy.com_60-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-streeteasy.com-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As in other parts of the city, some schools require students pass through metal detectors and swipe ID cards to enter school buildings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Nevertheless, since 1982 the community has been home to the
       <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Center_for_Science_and_Mathematics" title="Manhattan Center for Science and Mathematics">
        Manhattan Center for Science and Mathematics
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which replaced Benjamin Franklin High School, a school which had the smallest graduating class in the city at the time of its closing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Among the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charter_school_(New_York)" title="Charter school (New York)">
        public charter schools
       </a>
       in East Harlem are Success Academy Harlem 2 of
       <a href="/wiki/Success_Academy_Charter_Schools" title="Success Academy Charter Schools">
        Success Academy Charter Schools
       </a>
       , the Harlem Village Academy, East Harlem Scholars Academies, and the DREAM Charter School.
      </p>
      <p>
       Major medical care providers in the East Harlem area include
       <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Hospital_Center" title="Metropolitan Hospital Center">
        Metropolitan Hospital Center
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mount_Sinai_Hospital,_New_York" title="Mount Sinai Hospital, New York">
        Mount Sinai Hospital
       </a>
       , which serve residents of East Harlem and the Upper East Side.
       <a href="/wiki/North_General_Hospital" title="North General Hospital">
        North General Hospital
       </a>
       , which formerly served the area as well, is now closed. Many of the graduates of the
       <a href="/wiki/Icahn_School_of_Medicine_at_Mount_Sinai" title="Icahn School of Medicine at Mount Sinai">
        Icahn School of Medicine at Mount Sinai
       </a>
       do local public health work, including work on
       <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">
        asthma
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">
        diabetes
       </a>
       , unsafe drinking water,
       <a href="/wiki/Lead_paint" title="Lead paint">
        lead paint
       </a>
       and infectious diseases.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">
        Economy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Harlem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Economy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The neighborhood is home to one of the few major television studios north of midtown,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Metropolis at 106th Street and
       <a href="/wiki/Park_Avenue" title="Park Avenue">
        Park Avenue
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       where shows such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_Entertainment_Television" title="Black Entertainment Television">
        BET
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/106_%26_Park" title="106 &amp; Park">
         106 &amp; Park
        </a>
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Chappelle%27s_Show" title="Chappelle's Show">
         Chappelle's Show
        </a>
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       have been produced. PRdream.com, a web site on the history and culture of Puerto Ricans, founded a media gallery and digital film studio called MediaNoche in 2003. It presents technology-based art on Park Avenue and 102nd Street, providing exhibition space and residencies for artists and filmmakers, and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Webcasting" title="Webcasting">
        webcasting
       </a>
       events.
      </p>

         (born 1970), abstract expressionist painter

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">
        In popular culture
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=East_Harlem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: In popular culture">

        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       <b>
        Music

       <li>

        </a>

About 100 people, including Rep.          Zentella, Ana Celia (1997).
         <i>
          Growing Up Bilingual: Puerto Rican Children in New York
         </i>
         (Blackwell Publishers).
        </li>
       </ul>
